The Third Argument: The Slippery Slope to Single Report
Third argument: if tawatur yields, single report yields (by parity), which is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument: if tawatur yields knowledge, then a single report would also yield knowledge by the same reasoning, which is absurd.

Core Idea
If a group yields knowledge, then each smaller group also yields, down to one.

The third argument uses the same principle as the second: the ruling of each level is the same as the level before. By extending the chain, the deniers argue that if tawatur yields knowledge, then even a single report must yield knowledge, which is false. This passage presents that implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: 'لو أوجب التواتر العلم لأوجبه خبر الواحد'.
The argument is a conditional: if tawatur yields, then single report yields.
2
Understand the implication
The passage explains the implication using the same principle from the second argument: the ruling of each nature is the ruling of what is before it. This creates a chain from many down to one.
The marginal note gives an example: if 100 yields, then 99 yields, then 98, etc., down to 1.
3
Note the conclusion
The consequent (single report yields knowledge) is false by consensus. Therefore, the antecedent (tawatur yields knowledge) must be false.
This is a reductio argument: assuming tawatur yields leads to an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة (السادسة : ) من منكري البعثة ( من قال : العلم بحصول المعجز لا يمكن لمن لم يشاهده إلا بالتواتر ولكنه لا يفيد العلم ) فلا يحصل العلم بنبوة أحد لمن لم يشاهد ،معجزة، وإنما قلنا : إن التواتر لا يفيد العلم ( لوجوه : الأول : أهل التواتر يجوز الكذب على كل واحد منهم فكذا الكل يجوز عليه الكذب ( إذ ليس كذب الكل إلا كذب كل واحد. الثاني : إن حكم كل (طبقة ) من طبقات أعداد الرواة ( حكم ما قبلها بواحد فإن من جوز إفادة المائة للعلم جوز إفادة التسعة والتسعين له قطعاً، ولم يحصره ) أي العلم ( في عدد معين (و) أيضاً ( ادعاء الفرق ) بين العددين المذكورين في إفادة العلم ( تحكم) محض ، وإذا كان كذلك ( فلنفرض طبقة لا تفيده ) أي لا تفيد العلم قطعاً كاثنين مثلاً ( ثم نزيد عليه واحداً واحداً فلا يفيده) شيء من هذه المراتب (بالغاً ما بلغ المساواة كل منها لما قيل في عدم الإفادة. ( الثالث : لو أوجب التواتر العلم لأوجبه خبر الواحد واللازم منتف) اتفاقا ( بيان الملازمة أن التواتر لا يشترط فيه قوله : (بيان الملازمة أن التواتر (إلخ يمكن بيان الملازمة بمثل ما سبق من أن حكم كل طبيعة حكم ما قبلها فإذا أفاد المئة أفاد التسعة والتسعون، وإذا أفاد هذا أفاد الثمانية والتسعون إلى أن يبلغ الواحد .
The sixth group of deniers of prophethood (who said: Knowledge of the occurrence of a miracle is not possible for one who did not witness it except through tawatur, but it does not yield knowledge, so knowledge of the prophethood of anyone is not obtained for one who did not witness a miracle. And we said: Tawatur does not yield knowledge due to several reasons: First: It is possible for each one of the people of tawatur to lie, so similarly it is possible for the whole group to lie, because the lie of the whole is nothing but the lie of each individual. Second: The ruling of each (level) of the levels of the number of narrators is the ruling of what is one less than it. For if one permits that a hundred yields knowledge, one permits that ninety-nine yields it definitely, and it is not restricted to a specific number. And also claiming a difference between the two mentioned numbers in yielding knowledge is pure arbitrariness. And if this is so, then let us suppose a level that does not yield it, definitely cut off, like two, then we add one by one, and none of these levels yields it, no matter how far it goes, because each is equal to what was said about non-yielding. Third: If tawatur necessitated knowledge, then a single report would necessitate it, and the consequent is false by consensus. The explanation of the implication is that tawatur does not require... (marginal note: (The explanation of the implication that tawatur) can be made by the same reasoning as before that the ruling of each nature is the ruling of what is before it. So if a hundred yields, then ninety-nine yields, and if this yields, then eighty-eight yields, until reaching one.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of the sixth group who deny that tawatur (mass transmission) yields knowledge of a miracle one did not witness. They give three arguments: (1) Since each individual can lie, the whole group can lie, as the whole is composed of individuals. (2) If tawatur yields knowledge only from a certain number, it is arbitrary; if from any number, then adding individuals gradually would never reach certainty. (3) If tawatur yielded knowledge, then a single report would also, which is false. The marginal note shows how to establish the implication: by the same logic used earlier, that if a larger number yields, then each smaller number yields by parity, eventually reaching one.

Key Terms
خبر واحد
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawatur is completely different from a single report; they have no connection.
Correction: The deniers argue that by parity of reasoning, tawatur and single report are connected; if one yields knowledge, so should the other.

Checkpoint
What is the false consequent in the third argument?
A) Tawatur yields knowledge
B) A single report yields knowledge
C) Miracles occur
D) The chain is broken

Key Takeaway
The third argument reduces tawatur to absurdity by showing it would imply certain knowledge from a single report, which is false by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deniers of tawatur use a slippery slope argument to undermine certainty in mutawatir reports. Have you ever let hypothetical 'what if' scenarios shake your confidence in what you know for certain from the Qur'an and Sunnah? What would it take for you to reject a belief that all Muslims have unanimously held?
?
This argument forces us to examine the foundations of our knowledge. When you hear a seemingly logical argument that contradicts a core Islamic belief, do you immediately reject the belief or do you examine the premises? Reflect on your own intellectual humility: are you willing to accept that your reasoning might be flawed, or do you cling to your assumptions even when they lead to absurd conclusions?

Response to the First Objection: Denial of Equivalence
Ash'ari denial of equivalence of whole and parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the first objection, which denies that the whole is equivalent to its parts using an analogy of collective strength.

Core Idea
The whole is stronger than the sum of its parts.

The first objection claimed that if each individual report yields knowledge, then the whole group necessarily yields knowledge. The Ash'ari response challenges this equivalence by pointing out that the whole can have properties not reducible to its parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the structure of the response.
The passage begins with 'وجواب الأول' and ends with the analogy.
2
Identify the objection
Understand what the first objection claims: that the ruling of the whole should equal the ruling of each part.
3
Understand the response
The response denies equivalence and gives an analogy using ten people moving something that each cannot move alone.
This shows the whole can have properties not in the parts.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this analogy is used and how it supports the Ash'ari position on tawatur.
The analogy is empirical and does not invoke divine action directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجواب الأول : منع مساواة حكم الكل) من حيث هو كل لحكم كل واحد لما نرى من قوة العشرة على تحريك ما لا يقوى عليه كل واحد و) . جواب ( الثاني أن حصول العلم عنده ) أي عند التواتر ( عندنا) معاشر الأشاعرة إنما هو ( بخلق الله تعالى إياه وقد يخلقه بعدد دون عدد فلا نسلم تساوي طبقات الأعداد في احتمال الكذب وعدم إفادته العلم ( كيف وأنه ( أي حصول العلم بطريق تواتر الأخبار ( يختلف بالوقائع والمخبرين والسامعين) فقد يحصل العلم في واقعة بعدد مخصوص ولا يحصل به في واقعة أخرى وقد يحصل بأخبار جماعة مخصوصين ولا يحصل بأخبار جماعة أخرى تساويهم في العدد وكذا يحصل العلم لسامع من عدد ولا يحصل لسامع آخر من ذلك العدد ( و ) الجواب عن الثالث : أما عندنا فلانه ) أي العلم عقيب التواتر ( بخلق الله ) فقد يخلقه بعد أخبار عدد دون خبر واحد منفرد فلا يكون الخبر الأخير موجباً له ( وأما عند الحكماء والمعتزلة فلان الأخبار) الصادرة
And the answer to the first (objection): The denial of the equivalence of the ruling of the whole as a whole to the ruling of each individual, due to what we see of the strength of ten in moving what each individual cannot move. And the answer to the second (objection): That the occurrence of knowledge through it, i.e., through tawatur, with us, the community of Ash'aris, is only through God's creation of it, and He may create it with a (certain) number without (another) number. So we do not affirm the equality of layers of numbers in the possibility of lying and their not yielding knowledge. Moreover, the occurrence of knowledge through the way of tawatur differs according to events, reporters, and hearers. For knowledge may occur in one event with a specific number and not occur in another event with it, and may occur with the reports of specific groups and not occur with the reports of another group equal in number, and similarly knowledge may occur for one hearer from a number and not occur for another hearer from that number. And the answer to the third (objection): As for us, because knowledge subsequent to tawatur is by God's creation; He may create it after the reports of a number without a single isolated report, so the last report is not the cause of it. As for the philosophers and Mu'tazilites, because the (successive) reports...
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage gives the responses to the first three objections. The response to the first objection denies that the whole is equivalent to its parts; just as ten people can move something that each individual cannot, the whole group has a strength not reducible to individuals. The response to the second objection invokes the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism: God creates knowledge immediately, not through any inherent property of numbers. He may create knowledge after a certain number of reports but not after another, so layers are not equal in potential. The response to the third objection distinguishes between the Ash'ari position (God creates knowledge after a number of reports, not necessarily after the last single report) and the position of the philosophers and Mu'tazilites (who hold that the successive reports themselves are the cause, but the text cuts off before completing this point).

التواتر
حكم الكل
حكم كل واحد

Checkpoint

A) A group of ten moving something
B) A single person moving something
C) A number of reporters
D) A chain of narrators

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the first objection denies that the whole is equivalent to its parts, using the analogy of collective strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a collective truth because individual sources seemed weak? When has the testimony of a group (like a community or scholars) convinced you of something you initially doubted? Will you now reevaluate your default skepticism of gathered reports?
?
The analogy of ten people moving a rock shows that strength emerges from unity. In your own life, where have you underestimated the power of collective action in matters of faith (e.g., communal prayer, seeking knowledge in a group)? What will you do to stop relying solely on your own strength?
?
This lesson defends the legitimacy of tawatur as a source of certainty. Is there a truth about Islam that you hold with certainty based on tawatur? If so, how does that certainty affect your daily actions? If not, what missing link prevents you from accepting it? What will you do about it today?

Response to the Second Objection: Divine Creation of Knowledge
Ash'ari denial of equal layers of tawatur numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'aris explain the varying efficacy of tawatur numbers through divine creation.

Core Idea
God creates knowledge directly; numbers are not inherently causes.

The second objection assumed that all layers of numbers have equal potential to yield knowledge. The Ash'ari response invokes occasionalism: God creates knowledge after certain numbers but not others, so layers are not equal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the denial of equality and the mention of differences.
The passage is longer; take note of the two main points.
2
Understand occasionalism
Recognize that knowledge is created by God, not caused by numbers.
This is key to the Ash'ari metaphysical framework.
3
Note variations
The response highlights that knowledge varies by event, reporters, and hearers.
This shows empirical evidence against equality.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this response strengthen the Ash'ari position against the objection?
It shows that numbers are not the real cause; God is.

Response to the Third Objection: Incomplete
Ash'ari and philosophical responses to third objection (incomplete).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and philosophical/Mu'tazilite positions on the cause of knowledge from tawatur, and note the incompleteness of the source.

Core Idea
Ash'aris attribute knowledge to divine creation; others to the reports themselves.

The third objection concerns the mechanism by which the last report in a tawatur chain yields knowledge. The passage begins the response, contrasting the Ash'ari view with that of the philosophers and Mu'tazilites, but cuts off before completing their position.

The Seventh Group: Denial of Prophethood's Occurrence
Presentation of the seventh group's argument and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of those who accept the possibility of prophethood but deny its actual occurrence due to perceived irrationalities in revealed laws.

Core Idea
The laws of prophets are judged irrational by human reason—and therefore rejected.

The seventh group of skeptics does not deny that prophethood could exist, but they argue that the actual laws brought by claimants of prophethood contain elements that contradict reason and wisdom. They claim this proves these laws are not from God. In this lesson, we will examine their specific examples and the underlying assumption that human reason can definitively judge divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the group's stance
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the group admits the possibility of prophethood but denies its occurrence. Their method is to examine the content of revealed laws.
The group is not denying that prophethood could happen; they are arguing that it did not happen based on evidence.
2
List the specific examples they provide
The passage lists several examples of laws they find irrational: slaughtering animals, fasting, forbidding pleasures, and arduous rituals. Write them down and consider why each is considered contrary to reason and wisdom.
These examples are meant to show that the laws cause harm without apparent benefit to God.
3
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Consider the group's assumption that human reason can fully judge what is wise and rational in divine commands. Is this assumption valid? How might a believer respond?
This step connects the lesson to broader theological debates about the role of reason in religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة ( السابعة : من اعترف بإمكان البعثة ومنع ،وقوعها، قالوا : تتعبنا الشرائع) التي أتى بها مدعو الرسالة ( فوجدناها مشتملة على ما لا يوافق العقل والحكمة فعلمنا أنها ليست من عند الله فلا يكون هناك بعثة ) وذلك ) الذي لا يوافق العقل والحكمة ) كإباحة بح الحيوان وإيلامه ) لمنفعة الأكل وغيره ( و ) إيجاب (تحمل الجوع والعطش في ) صوم ( أيام معينة والمنع من الملاذ التي بها صلاح البدن مع أنه لا منفعة في هذا المنع لله سبحانه وفيه مضار لعباده فيكون مخالفاً للحكمة وتكليف الأفعال الشاقة كطي الفيافي وكزيارة بعض المواضع والوقوف ببعض والسعي في بعض والطواف ببعض مع تمايلها ومضاهاة المجانين والصبيان في التعري عليه دائر بين
The seventh group: those who admitted the possibility of the mission (al-ba‘tha) and denied its occurrence. They said: We examined the laws (al-sharā’i‘) brought by the claimants of prophethood (al-risāla), and we found them containing what does not accord with reason and wisdom; so we knew that they are not from God, and thus there is no mission. And that which does not accord with reason and wisdom includes: permitting the slaughter of animals and causing them pain for the benefit of eating and other things; and imposing the bearing of hunger and thirst in fasting on specific days; and forbidding pleasures by which the body is improved, while there is no benefit in this prohibition for God, Exalted, and it contains harms for His servants, so it is contrary to wisdom; and the obligation of difficult actions such as traversing deserts, visiting certain places, standing at some, running at some, and circumambulating some, while they have inclinations and resemble madmen and children in uncovering—which about it goes back and forth
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage introduces the seventh group of skeptics regarding prophethood. They do not deny the theoretical possibility (imkān) of a divine mission (ba‘tha), but they deny its actual occurrence. Their argument is empirical: they claim to have examined the revealed laws (sharā’i‘) brought by those who claimed prophethood, and they found them to contain elements that contradict reason (‘aql) and wisdom (ḥikma). Therefore, they conclude these laws cannot be from God. The text lists specific examples: (1) permitting the slaughter of animals and causing them pain for human benefit; (2) imposing hunger and thirst through fasting on specific days; (3) forbidding pleasures that are beneficial for the body, which seems to cause harm without benefit to God; (4) requiring arduous rituals such as traveling across deserts, visiting specific places, standing, running, and circumambulating, which they compare to the actions of madmen and children. The passage cuts off, but the argument is that such laws are irrational and unwise, proving they are not divine.

Key Terms
بعثة
شرائع
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seventh group denies the possibility of prophethood altogether.
Correction: They actually admit the possibility (imkān) of prophethood but deny its actual occurrence (wuqū‘) based on their critique of revealed laws.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the seventh group against the actual occurrence of prophethood?
A) Prophethood is logically impossible
B) The revealed laws contain elements contrary to reason and wisdom
C) There is no need for prophethood in human society
D) The prophets were impostors lying about revelation

Key Takeaway
The seventh group's argument against actual prophethood is based on an empirical critique of revealed laws, not on a priori impossibility, and their examples challenge the wisdom of divine commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found a command of Allah—like fasting or giving up a pleasure—difficult to accept because it seemed unreasonable or harmful to you? In that moment, did you trust Allah's wisdom more than your own, or did you internally question the command like the seventh group? What specific examples from this lesson expose the weakness of relying on human reason alone?
?
The seventh group claimed that arduous acts of worship (like pilgrimage rituals) resemble the actions of madmen. When you perform such acts, do you ever feel they are pointless or embarrassing? How can you remind yourself that true wisdom lies in submission to Allah's command, even when it defies human logic? Commit to one change in your attitude toward a difficult worship this week.

Answering the Objection of Apparent Irrationalities in Religious Laws
Al-Iji presents the seventh group's objection and his two-part answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji refutes the objection that apparent irrationalities in religious laws disprove prophethood by distinguishing between perceived wisdom and actual wisdom, and by highlighting the known benefits of devotional acts.

Core Idea
Lack of perceived wisdom does not imply lack of wisdom; God's commands have hidden and manifest benefits.

The seventh group of objectors to prophethood claim that the laws brought by prophets contain elements that contradict reason and wisdom, thus they cannot be from God. They list several examples. Al-Iji responds by first conceding rational judgment but then pointing out that failing to perceive wisdom is not evidence of its absence. Moreover, these commands carry known benefits like soul training and increased spiritual reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its examples
Read the passage from the beginning of the seventh group to the end of the examples. Notice the various types of laws they claim contradict reason.
The examples include slaughter, fasting, pilgrimage rituals, and seemingly arbitrary rules.
2
Understand the core of the objection
Identify the logical structure: if laws contain irrational elements, they cannot be from God, so no prophethood. This is the target of the answer.
3
Analyze the first part of the answer
Al-Iji concedes rational judgment but says that failure to find wisdom does not prove its absence. Reflect on the distinction between 'not known' and 'non-existent'.
This is a key logical point.
4
Analyze the second part of the answer
Al-Iji lists known benefits: subjugation of the soul, breaking pride, and increase of trial (ibtilā'). See how these are actually beneficial.
These benefits turn the objection on its head.
5
Reflect and apply
Consider how this argument applies to modern critiques of religion. Why is it important to distinguish between perceived and real wisdom?
This connects the lesson to broader discussions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة ( السابعة : من اعترف بإمكان البعثة ومنع،وقوعها، قالوا: تتعبنا الشرائع) التي أتى بها مدعو الرسالة ( فوجدناها مشتملة على ما لا يوافق العقل والحكمة فعلمنا أنها ليست من عند الله فلا يكون هناك بعثة ) وذلك ) الذي لا يوافق العقل والحكمة ) كإباحة بح الحيوان وإيلامه ) لمنفعة الأكل وغيره ( و ) إيجاب (تحمل الجوع والعطش في ) صوم ( أيام معينة والمنع من الملاذ التي بها صلاح البدن مع أنه لا منفعة في هذا المنع لله سبحانه وفيه مضار لعباده فيكون مخالفاً للحكمة وتكليف الأفعال الشاقة كطي الفيافي وكزيارة بعض المواضع والوقوف ببعض والسعي في بعض والطواف ببعض مع تمايلها ومضاهاة المجانين والصبيان في التعري عليه دائر بين وكشف الرأس والرمي لا إلى مرمى وتقبيل حجر لا مزية له على سائر الأحجار، وكتحريم النظر إلى الحرة الشوهاء دون الأمة الحسناء وكحرمة أخذ الفضل في صفقة و جوازه في صفقتين) كان يبيع مد عجوة جيدة بمدين من عجوة رديئة فإنه ربا حرام وإن باعها بدرهم ثم اشترى به مدين من الرديئة كان حلالاً بلا شبهة (مع استوائهما ) أي الصفقة والصفقتين ) في المصالح والمفاسد ( من جميع الوجوه . ( والجواب بعد تسليم حكم العقل فيه بالحسن والقبح ووجوب الغرض في أفعاله تعالى ( فغايته ) أي غاية ما ذكرتموه ) عدم الوقوف على الحكمة في تلك الصور المذكورة ) ولا يلزم منه عدمها ) في نفس الأمر ( ولعل ) هناك ( مصلحة استأثر الله بالعلم بها على أن في التعبد بما لا تعلم حكمته تطويعاً للنفس الأبية وملكة (قهرها أي تصرف غلبتها الثابتة ( فيما فيه الحكمة وزيادة ابتلاء في التعرض للثواب والعقاب ) يعني أن النفس إذا علمت الحكمة والمصلحة في حكم انقادت له لأجل تلك المصلحة لا لمجرد امتثال حكم مولاها سيدها وكان عندها أنها ذات قوة ورسوخ في العلم فربما صارت بسبب ذلك معجية بنفسها فإذا تعبدت بما لا تعلم حكمته كان انقيادها امتثالاً مجرداً وانكسرت سورتها وإعجابها الثابت لها فيما علمت حكمته وأيضاً في التعبد زيادة ابتلاء في التكليف فإن النفس تأبى عمالاً تعلم مصلحته وكل ذلك حكمة ومصلحة حاصلة في الأحكام التعبدية ومعلومة لنا فلا يلزم خلو تلك الصور عن الحكمة والمصلحة المعتد بها المعلومة.
The seventh group: those who acknowledge the possibility of the prophetic mission but prevent its occurrence. They said: We examined the laws (sharā’i‘) brought by the claimant of the message, and we found them containing what does not agree with intellect and wisdom, so we knew that they are not from God, and thus there is no prophetic mission. And that (which does not agree with intellect and wisdom) includes: permitting the slaughter of animals and causing them pain for the benefit of eating and other things; and requiring the bearing of hunger and thirst in fasting on specific days, and forbidding pleasures by which the body is maintained, while there is no benefit in this prohibition to God (glorified is He) and there are harms to His servants, so it contradicts wisdom; and requiring difficult actions like crossing deserts, visiting certain places, standing at some, running at some, and circumambulating some, while inclining and resembling the insane and children in uncovering the head, throwing without a target, kissing a stone with no superiority over other stones; and forbidding looking at a free ugly woman while allowing a beautiful slave woman; and forbidding taking a surplus in one transaction while allowing it in two transactions: if one sells a good sa‘ of dates for two sa‘s of bad dates, it is usury (ribā) and forbidden; but if he sells it for a dirham then buys with it two sa‘s of bad dates, it is lawful without doubt, while the two transactions are equal in benefits and harms from all aspects. And the answer, after conceding the judgment of reason regarding good and bad and the necessity of purpose in the acts of God (exalted), is that the utmost of what you mentioned is the failure to arrive at the wisdom in those mentioned cases, and that does not entail its absence in reality. Perhaps there is a benefit that God has reserved knowledge of. Moreover, in being bound by acts whose wisdom is not known, there is a subjugation of the proud soul and a quality of subduing it (i.e., a disposition of its constant dominance?) in that which has wisdom, and an increase of trial in exposing oneself to reward and punishment. That is, if the soul knows the wisdom and benefit in a ruling, it submits to it because of that benefit, not merely to obey the command of its Master, and it considers itself to have strength and firmness in knowledge, so perhaps it becomes conceited. But if it is bound by what it does not know its wisdom, its submission becomes pure obedience, and its pride and self-admiration that it had regarding what it knew of wisdom are broken. Also, in being bound there is an increase of trial in obligation, for the soul refuses acts whose benefit it knows. All of that is wisdom and benefit present in devotional rulings (al-aḥkām al-ta‘abbudiyya) and known to us, so it does not follow that those cases are devoid of the recognized, known wisdom and benefit.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer. The objection comes from a group (the seventh) who accept that prophethood is possible but deny that it has actually occurred. Their evidence is that they find the shar‘ī laws (sharā’i‘) brought by the claimants contain elements that seem to contradict reason and wisdom. They list examples: permitting slaughter of animals and causing them pain; requiring fasting with hunger and thirst; forbidding pleasures that maintain the body; difficult rituals like pilgrimage (crossing deserts, visiting places, standing, running, circumambulating, uncovering the head, throwing pebbles, kissing the Black Stone); seemingly arbitrary rules like forbidding looking at an ugly free woman but allowing a beautiful slave woman; and the rule about usury where selling good dates for two measures of bad dates in one transaction is forbidden but selling for dirhams and then buying them in two transactions is allowed, even though the outcomes are economically equivalent. The answer begins by conceding the rational judgment of good and bad (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ) and that God's acts are purposeful. However, the objection only shows that humans cannot perceive the wisdom in these commands, not that wisdom is absent. There may be hidden benefits known only to God. Moreover, there are known benefits: the subjugation of the proud soul, breaking its arrogance, and increasing the trial (ibtilā’) in worship. The soul tends to obey when it sees benefit, but pure obedience without knowing the wisdom is a greater test and humbles the soul. Thus, these devotional rulings (aḥkām ta‘abbudiyya) are not devoid of wisdom and benefit; indeed, we can understand these benefits.

بعثة (ba‘tha)
شرائع (shara’i‘)
حكمة (hikma)
مصلحة (maslaha)
تعبد (ta‘abbud)
تطويع النفس (tatwi‘ al-nafs)
ابتلاء (ibtilā’)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A religious ruling that appears irrational cannot be from God.
Correction: Al-Iji argues that failing to perceive wisdom does not prove its absence, and there may be hidden or manifest benefits.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the answer to the seventh group's objection?
A) The wise person can always see the wisdom in every ruling.
B) Failure to perceive the wisdom does not mean it is absent, and there are known benefits in devotional rulings.
C) The examples given are actually in harmony with reason.
D) The soul should always seek to understand wisdom before obeying.

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji refutes the objection by distinguishing between perceived and actual wisdom, and by revealing the hidden benefits of devotional acts for the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command of Allah that seems irrational or pointless to you, do you submit purely out of obedience, or do you hold back until you see the benefit? Can you honestly identify a specific ruling you find difficult to accept?
?
The passage says that knowing the wisdom behind a command can breed pride in the soul. In your own acts of worship, are you more motivated by understanding the reason or by the sheer command of your Lord? Which one humbles you more?
?
If all the wisdom behind every command were revealed, your soul might obey for the benefit and become conceited. Are you willing to obey Allah even when it seems harmful to you? What would that obedience cost you, and what would it produce in your heart?

The First Proof for Muhammad's Prophethood: Claim and Miracle
Al-Iji presents the first and main proof for Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of Muhammad's prophethood is established through his widely transmitted claim and the miracle of the Quran demonstrated by the challenge and failure to imitate.

Core Idea
Prophethood proven by undeniable claim and an unmatched miracle.

This passage presents the primary proof for Muhammad's prophethood, combining two elements: his claim to prophethood, which is so widely transmitted as to be undeniable, and the miracle of the Quran, which he challenged others to imitate and they failed. Together, they form a decisive argument for his prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof's structure
Notice that the proof has two independent pillars: the claim (da'wa) and the miracle (mu'jiza). Read the passage to see how Al-Iji introduces both.
The claim is said to be mutawatir, reaching the level of direct observation.
2
Grasp the claim's transmission
Al-Iji states that Muhammad's claim to prophethood is mutawatir, meaning it has been transmitted by so many people at each level that certainty is achieved. This makes denial impossible.
Compare mutawatir to isolated reports (khabar ahad).
3
Analyze the Quran as miracle
The Quran is miraculous because Muhammad challenged (tahaddi) the Arabs to produce something like it, and they could not. The challenge itself is also mutawatir, with many Quranic verses explicitly issuing the challenge.
The verses cited are from Surah at-Tur and Surah Hud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الرابع : في إثبات نبوة محمد
وفيه مسالك : المسلك الأول : وهو العمدة أنه ادعى النبوة وظهرت المعجزة على يده أما الأولى فتواتره تواتراً ألحقه بالعيان والمشاهدة فلا مجال للإنكار فيها، ( وأما الثانية فمعجزة القرآن وغيره )
[ الكلام في القرآن ]
وكونه معجزاً أن نقول : تحدى به ولم يعارض فكان معجزاً ( أما أنه تحدى به فقد تواتر) بحيث لم يبق فيه شبهة ) وآيات التحدي كثيرة ) كقوله تعالى : فليأتوا بحديث مثله ﴾ [الطور : ٣٤] ، وقوله : فأتوا بعشر سور مثله مفتريات ﴾ [هود :١٣ ] ،
قوله : ( أما الأولى فمتواترة تواتراً ألحقه (بالعيان التأنيث في الموضعين باعتبار الدعوى وتذكير البارز في الحقه باعتبار الادعاء.
First Observatory - Fourth Purpose: On proving the prophethood of Muhammad. And it contains methods (masālik): The first method, which is the main one, is that he claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared at his hands. As for the first, it is mutawātir in a way that attaches it to direct sight and observation, so there is no room for denial in it. (As for the second, it is the miracle of the Qur’an and others.) [Discussion on the Qur’an] And its being miraculous is that we say: He challenged with it and it was not opposed, so it was miraculous. (As for the fact that he challenged with it, it is mutawātir) to the point that there is no doubt about it. And the verses of challenge are many, such as His saying (Exalted): 'Then let them produce a recital like it' [al-Ṭūr: 34], and His saying: 'Then bring ten surahs like it, fabricated' [Hūd: 13]. His saying: (As for the first, it is mutawātir in a way that attaches it to direct sight. The feminine [pronouns] in the two places refer to the claim, and the masculine in the attached pronoun refers to the claim [itself].)
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth purpose of the first observatory: proving the prophethood of Muhammad. It presents the first method (al-maslak al-awwal), described as the main one. The proof consists of two parts: 1) Muhammad's claim to prophethood, which is mutawātir (massively transmitted) to the level of direct sight and observation, and thus undeniable. 2) The appearance of a miracle at his hands, specifically the miracle of the Qur'an and others. The discussion then focuses on the Qur'an as a miracle: it is miraculous because Muhammad challenged (taḥaddā) the Arabs to produce something like it, and they failed to do so. The fact that he challenged with the Qur'an is also mutawātir, leaving no doubt. Verses of challenge are cited: 'Then let them produce a recital like it' (52:34) and 'Then bring ten surahs like it, fabricated' (11:13). The passage ends with a grammatical note explaining that the feminine pronouns in the preceding text refer to the claim (al-da‘wā), while the masculine pronoun in 'alḥaqahu' (attached it) refers to the claim itself (al-ad‘ā').

Key Terms
نبوة (nubuwwa)
معجزة (mu‘jiza)
تحدي (tahaddi)
تواتر (tawatur)
عيان (‘iyān)
قرآن (Qur’ān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The miracle of the Quran is merely its literary excellence or beauty.
Correction: The argument is based on the successful challenge (tahaddi) and the failure of the Arabs to produce anything like it, not subjective appreciation. The challenge itself is historically verified through tawatur.

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence for the miracle of the Qur'an according to this passage?
A) Its scientific accuracy.
B) The fact that Muhammad challenged others to produce something like it and they could not.
C) The beauty of its language.
D) The agreement of all Muslims that it is a miracle.

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's prophethood is established by his widely known claim and the unsurpassed miracle of the Quran, which he challenged others to imitate and they failed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly accept that Muhammad's claim to prophethood is as certain as if you had seen and heard him yourself? If not, what is holding you back from that conviction?
?
The Quran's challenge to produce a single surah like it remains unanswered. How does that reality confront the way you approach the Quran—do you treat it as merely a book or as the living miracle it is?
?
If someone asked you to explain why the Quran is a miracle using only the argument from the source (challenge and failure), could you do so? If not, what is preventing you from owning this proof personally?

Prophethood

The Main Proof of Prophethood: Claim and Unchallenged Miracle
Proof of Muhammad's prophethood from his claim and the Qur'anic challenge with no opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's claim to prophethood and the Qur'an's challenge with no opposition constitute a definitive proof of his prophethood.

Core Idea
The Prophet's claim and the Qur'an's unopposed challenge together form the strongest proof of prophethood.

This passage presents the first and primary approach to proving prophethood: that the Prophet claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared at his hand. The claim is so widely transmitted that it equals direct observation, and the miracle—especially the Qur'an—is proven by the challenge to produce something like it and the historical fact that no one succeeded. This lesson unpacks this argument and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read and grasp the main argument
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice that the argument has two pillars: the claim of prophethood (mutawātir) and the miracle (the Qur'an and others).
2
Analyze the proof for the Qur'an's miracle
Understand that the Qur'an's miraculous nature is proven by two facts: the Prophet challenged the Arabs to produce something similar (mutawātir verses), and no one succeeded in opposing it. The lack of opposition must be true because if it had occurred, it would have been transmitted due to the large number of eager opponents.
3
Reflect on why this is the main proof
Consider why the author calls this approach 'al-ʿumdah' (the main one). It relies on widely transmitted historical facts that are undeniable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الأول: وهو العمدة أنه ادعى النبوة وظهرت المعجزة على يده أما الأولى فتواتره تواتراً ألحقه بالعيان والمشاهدة فلا مجال للإنكار فيها، وأما الثانية فمعجزة القرآن وغيره. الكلام في القرآن وكونه معجزاً أن نقول: تحدى به ولم يعارض فكان معجزاً. أما أنه تحدى به فقد تواتر بحيث لم يبق فيه شبهة. وآيات التحدي كثيرة كقوله تعالى: ﴿فليأتوا بحديث مثله﴾ [الطور: 34]، وقوله: ﴿فأتوا بعشر سور مثله مفتريات﴾ [هود:13]، وقوله: ﴿فأتوا بسورة من مثله﴾ [البقرة:23]. وأما أنه لم يعارض فلأنه لو عورض لتواتر لأنه مما توفرت الدواعي إلى نقله سيما والخصوم أكثر عدداً من حصى البطحاء وأحرص الناس على إشاعة ما يبطل دعواه. وأما أنه حينئذ يكون معجزاً فقد مر فيما سبق من بيان حقيقة المعجزة وشرائطها. والكلام على هذه الطريقة سؤالاً وجواباً يعلم من الفصل المتقدم فإن الشبه التي أوردها منكرو البعثة يمكن إيرادها هاهنا وأجوبتها تعلم من هناك أيضاً فلا حاجة بنا إلى إعادتها. قوله: (فأتوا بسورة من مثله) قد ذكرنا في حواشي المطول أن قوله من مثله إن جعل لغواً متعلقاً بفاتوا فالضمير المجرور لعبدنا لا للموصول في مما نزلنا وإن جعل مستقراً صفة لسورة فإما راجع إليه أو إلى الموصول فليرجع إليه.
The first approach—and it is the main one—is that he claimed prophethood and a miracle appeared at his hand. As for the first, his claim is transmitted with such widespread transmission as to bring it to the level of direct sight and observation, so there is no room for denial. As for the second, it is the miracle of the Qur'an and others. The discussion on the Qur'an: and it being miraculous is that we say: he challenged with it and it was not opposed, so it was miraculous. As for that he challenged with it, it has been transmitted with such widespread transmission that there remains no doubt. And the verses of challenge are many, such as His statement: 'Then let them produce a discourse like it' (52:34), and His statement: 'Then bring ten forged chapters like it' (11:13), and His statement: 'Then bring a chapter like it' (2:23). And as for that it was not opposed, because if it had been opposed, it would have been transmitted due to the numerous motives for its transmission, especially since the opponents are more numerous than the pebbles of the valley and are the most eager people to spread anything that invalidates his claim. And as for that it is then miraculous, it has passed earlier in the explanation of the reality of a miracle and its conditions. And the discussion along this method, in terms of questions and answers, is known from the preceding chapter, for the doubts that the deniers of the mission have raised can be raised here, and their answers are also known from there, so we have no need to repeat them. His statement: 'Then bring a chapter like it'—we have mentioned in the footnotes of al-Mutawwal that the phrase 'like it,' if it is considered a dependent clause connected to 'bring,' then the pronominal object refers to 'Our servant,' not to the relative pronoun in 'what we have sent down,' and if it is considered a circumstantial qualifier describing 'chapter,' then it either refers to him or to the relative pronoun; so refer to it.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a proof of Muhammad's prophethood through the first approach: his claim and the accompanying miracle. The author states that the claim is mutawātir (massively transmitted), reaching the level of direct observation, so it cannot be denied. The miracle is primarily the Qur'an, but also other miracles. For the Qur'an, the proof is that he challenged the Arabs to produce something like it, and they failed to oppose it. The challenge is also mutawātir through many verses. If it had been opposed, the opposition would have been transmitted because there were strong motives for transmission, especially since the opponents were numerous and eager to refute him. Since no opposition was transmitted, it is a miracle. The author then refers to previous discussions for the detailed definition of miracle and for answering doubts. Finally, a grammatical note on the verse 'bring a chapter like it' clarifies the possible interpretations of the phrase 'like it.'

Key Terms
المعجزة
التحدي
التواتر
العيان
الخصوم
الاعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Qur'an's inimitability is subjective and cannot be proven historically.
Correction: The author demonstrates that it is proven by the historical fact of the challenge and the failure to oppose it, which are established by tawātur.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why would any opposition to the Qur'anic challenge have been transmitted?
A) Because of the large number of Muslims.
B) Because the opponents were so numerous and eager to spread anything that would invalidate Muhammad's claim.
C) Because the Qur'an itself records all opposition.
D) Because the challenge was only a verbal claim.

Key Takeaway
The strongest proof of prophethood is the Prophet's claim, verified by tawātur, and the Qur'an, whose miraculous nature is proven by the challenge and the historical absence of any successful opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet's claim is as certain as if you had seen him yourself. Why do you still live as though his message is optional? What would change today if you truly believed that?
?
The opponents were desperate to disprove the Qur'an but failed. Have you ever honestly examined the Qur'an's challenge in your own heart? What stops you from accepting it fully?
?
If the lack of opposition to the Qur'an is a proof of its divine origin, what does your own lack of response to that proof say about your sincerity? Will you answer the challenge today?

The Relativity of Eloquence Objection
Presents the objection of relative eloquence and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why relative superiority in eloquence does not constitute a miracle, and how this objection is refuted.

Core Idea
Being the best of your time isn't a miracle, or everyone would be a miracle-worker.

One objection against the Qur'an's inimitability is that Muhammad was simply the most eloquent speaker of his era, not that his speech was divinely unique. But if that were enough, then any person who excels in any craft would be performing a miracle. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim: Muhammad was the most eloquent of his time.
Notice the key phrase 'فلعل محمداً كان أفصح أهل عصره'.
2
Understand the refutation
See how the author extends the logic: if relative superiority were a miracle, then every person who excels in any craft in any age would be a miracle-worker. This is absurd.
The phrase 'وهو ضروري البطلان' indicates the conclusion is necessarily false.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why a miracle must be absolutely inimitable, not merely better than contemporaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الرابع في إثبات نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم تلك المراتب لم يصل إليها غيره في عصره وإن أمكن أن يفوقه شخص آخر في عصر آخر ( فلعل محمداً كان أفصح أهل عصره ) فأتى بكلام عجز عن مثله أهل زمانه ) ولو كان ذلك معجزاً لكان ( ما أتى به كل من فاق أقرانه في صناعة) من الصناعات في عصر من الأعصار ( معجزاً وهو ضروري البطلان، وأما مذهب القاضي فلأن ضم غير المعجز إلى مثله لا يصيره معجزاً
First observation - Fourth objective in proving the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him. Those ranks were not reached by anyone else in his time, even though it is possible that another person could surpass him in another time. (So perhaps Muhammad was the most eloquent of his era) and he produced speech that the people of his time were unable to match. And if that had been a miracle, then (what every person who excelled his peers in any craft) from among the crafts in any age (would be a miracle) and that is necessarily false. And as for the view of al-Qadi (the judge), it is because combining a non-miracle with its like does not make it a miracle.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct objections against the argument that the Qur'an's eloquence is miraculous. The first objection (lines 1-5) posits that Muhammad's unmatched eloquence was merely relative to his time; he was the most eloquent of his era, but this does not entail a supernatural miracle, since another person in a different age could surpass him. The response shows the absurd consequence: if relative superiority in a skill were a miracle, then every person who outperforms his contemporaries in any craft would be performing a miracle, which is plainly false. The second objection (lines 6-7), attributed to al-Qadi, argues that attaching a non-miraculous element to another non-miraculous element does not transform it into a miracle. This likely addresses the notion that the Qur'an's eloquence, combined with other features like predictions or lack of contradiction, becomes miraculous; al-Qadi denies that such combination can produce a miracle.

Key Terms
أفصح
معجز
صناعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that being the best in your field is enough to be a miracle.
Correction: A miracle requires absolute inimitability, not mere superiority over contemporaries.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation of the relative eloquence objection?
A) It proves Muhammad was not eloquent.
B) It would make every person who excels in any craft a miracle-worker, which is absurd.
C) It ignores the predictions in the Qur'an.
D) It shows that no one can surpass Muhammad in any age.

Key Takeaway
Relative eloquence does not prove a miracle; a miracle must be absolutely inimitable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking of the Qur'an as just a remarkable book among many, rather than the inimitable word of Allah? How would your relationship with it change if you truly believed no one can produce its like?
?
You have talents in some craft or skill—do you ever feel a sense of pride as if they were miraculous? How does the reductio in this lesson humble you regarding your abilities?
?
When you hear the objection that Muhammad (peace be upon him) was simply the most eloquent of his time, does any doubt cross your heart? How will you use this logical refutation to strengthen your conviction the next time such doubt arises?

Al-Qadi's Argument on Combining Non-Miracles
Presents al-Qadi's objection that combining non-miracles does not yield a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's objection that combining non-miraculous elements does not produce a miracle.

Core Idea
Adding non-miracles together doesn't make a miracle.

A second objection to the Qur'an's inimitability comes from al-Qadi, who argues that if individual aspects of the Qur'an are not miraculous, combining them doesn't create a miracle. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Qadi's statement
Read the Arabic and translation: 'ضم غير المعجز إلى مثله لا يصيره معجزاً'
This is a concise logical principle.
2
Consider the implication
Think about what this means for arguments that combine multiple non-miraculous features of the Qur'an to claim overall inimitability.
The objector claims that the whole is no more than the sum of its parts.
3
Evaluate the objection
Reflect on whether al-Qadi's principle is sound and how it might be countered.
This lesson does not provide a counter, only presents the objection.

?

?
Al-Qadi's logic strips away our tendency to inflate the value of our efforts. Where in your life have you been combining 'non-miracles'—perhaps your knowledge, your charity, or your worship—and expecting them to tip the scales as if they were a miracle? What would it mean for you to rely entirely on Allah's mercy instead of the accumulation of your works?

First Objection: Undefined Threshold for Miraculous Predictions
First objection: undefined frequency for miraculous predictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the Qur'an's predictions being a miracle: that occasional predictions can come from saints or coincidence, and the undefined repetition threshold makes it impossible to know if the Qur'an meets the criterion.

Core Idea
A single prediction is not enough; we need a defined frequency to call it a miracle.

The first objection challenges the very basis of declaring predictions miraculous. It argues that predictions can occur as divine favors for saints or mere coincidences without breaking natural laws. Only when predictions recur repeatedly do they become breaches of custom, but the exact number of repetitions needed is unspecified. This raises a problem: how can we know the Qur'an has reached that level? In this lesson, we will unpack this subtle argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the logical flow: occasional predictions are permissible, but only repetition makes them miraculous, yet the threshold is undefined.
Notice the parentheses indicate explanatory insertions by the commentator.
2
Understand key terms
Identify the terms: karamah (divine favor), wali (saint), kharaq al-'adah (breach of custom). How do they relate to the objection?
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Identify the core problem
The core problem is that the number of repetitions needed for a miracle is not fixed. Ask yourself: why does this make it hard to confirm the Qur'an's miraculousness in predictions?
This leads to the question: 'How can one know?'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإخبار بالغيب فلوجوه : الأول : أنه جائز كرامة للولي وعلى سبيل الاتفاق أيضا بلا خرق عادة كما في المرة والمرتين (إلا أن يتكرر ) ذلك الإخبار ( إلى أن يصير) خارقاً للعادة فيصير حينئذ ( معجزاً ومراتبه ) أي مراتب التكرر إلى حد الإعجاز (غير مضبوطة) بعدد معين (فكيف يعلم بلوغ القرآن ) في الإخبار بالغيب ( مرتبة الإعجاز . الثاني : أنه يقع ذلك الإخبار مكررا (من المنجمين والكهنة كما دل عليه التسامع والتجربة ) وليس بمعجز اتفاقاً . الثالث : أنه يلزم حينئذ أن لا يكون ما خلا عنه ) أي عن الإخبار بالغيب ( من القرآن معجزاً) فيخرج أكثر القرآن عن صفة الإعجاز وهو باطل
As for predictions of the unseen, there are objections: First: It is permissible as a karamah (miracle) for a saint (wali) and also by coincidence without breaking the norm, as in once or twice (unless that prediction is repeated until it becomes a breach of custom, then it becomes a miracle, and its ranks) i.e., the ranks of repetition to the level of miracle (are not fixed) by a specific number, so how can one know that the Qur'an has reached the level of miraculousness in predicting the unseen? Second: Such predictions occur repeatedly from astrologers and soothsayers, as is known by hearsay and experience, and they are not miraculous by agreement. Third: It would then follow that what is devoid of predictions of the unseen in the Qur'an would not be miraculous, so most of the Qur'an would exit the attribute of inimitability, which is false.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the claim that the Qur'an's predictions of the unseen constitute a miracle. The first objection argues that occasional predictions can be a karamah (divine favor) for a saint or mere coincidence without breaking natural laws; only if predictions become frequent do they break custom and become miracles. However, the threshold for frequency is undefined, so it is impossible to know if the Qur'an has reached that level. The second objection points out that similar predictions are frequently made by astrologers and soothsayers, as established by common knowledge and experience, yet these are not considered miraculous by consensus. The third objection states that if only the predictions were miraculous, then the majority of the Qur'an that does not contain predictions would lose its miraculous status, which is untenable.

Key Terms
إخبار بالغيب
كرامة
ولي
خرق العادة
إعجاز
مراتب التكرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every prediction of the future is automatically a miracle.
Correction: The passage explains that predictions can be karamah or coincidence; only repeated predictions that breach custom become miracles, and the threshold is undefined.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem raised in the first objection?
A) Predictions are impossible for saints.
B) The number of repetitions needed for a miracle is not fixed.
C) The Qur'an has no predictions.
D) Predictions are always miraculous.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that without a clear frequency threshold, we cannot claim Qur'anic predictions are miraculous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an's predictions of the unseen, do you count how many have come true to convince yourself? Instead of relying on a number, can you let your heart be certain of its divine origin without demanding a threshold?
?
Have you ever experienced what you thought was a miracle in your life, only to later wonder if it was just coincidence? How does this objection about undefined frequency make you re-examine your own criteria for divine signs?
?
The objector says we cannot know the Qur'an reached the level of miraculousness because the ranks are not fixed. Do you find your faith shaken by such intellectual doubts? Will you actively seek to strengthen your certainty through deeper reflection on the Qur'an's unique content, not just its predictions?

Second Objection: Astrologers and Soothsayers Also Predict
Second objection: non-prophetic predictions as counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that similar predictions are made by astrologers and soothsayers, yet are not considered miracles, casting doubt on the uniqueness of the Qur'an's predictions.

Core Idea
If others can do it, it's not unique enough to be a miracle.

The second objection draws attention to a common empirical reality: predictions of the unseen are not exclusive to prophets. Astrologers and soothsayers have long made such claims, and their predictions are well-known through hearsay and experience. These are not considered miraculous by general agreement. This poses a challenge: if the Qur'an's predictions are similar in nature, why would they be miraculous? In this lesson, we explore this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the reference to astrologers and soothsayers as examples of non-miraculous prediction.
The phrase 'as is known by hearsay and experience' grounds the objection in common observation.
2
Compare with the first objection
Think about how this objection differs: the first focused on the frequency problem; this one focuses on the existence of similar predictions from non-prophetic sources.
The objection relies on consensus that these predictions are not miracles.
3
Consider the implication
If predictions from other sources are not miraculous, then the Qur'an's predictions must have a distinguishing feature to be exceptional. Is that feature present?
This objection sets up a need to prove the uniqueness of the Qur'an's predictions.

Source Text

As for predictions of the unseen, there are objections: First: It is permissible as a karamah (miracle) for a saint (wali) and also by coincidence without breaking the norm, as in once or twice (unless that prediction is repeated until it becomes a breach of custom, then it becomes a miracle, and its ranks) i.e., the ranks of repetition to the level of miracle (are not fixed) by a specific number, so how can one know that the Qur'an has reached the level of miraculousness in predicting the unseen? Second: Such predictions occur repeatedly from astrologers and soothsayers, as is known by hearsay and experience, and they are not miraculous by agreement. Third: It would then follow that what is devoid of predictions of the unseen in the Qur'an would not be miraculous, so most of the Qur'an would exit the attribute of inimitability, which is false.

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the claim that the Qur'an's predictions of the unseen constitute a miracle. The first objection argues that occasional predictions can be a karamah (divine favor) for a saint or mere coincidence without breaking natural laws; only if predictions become frequent do they break custom and become miracles. However, the threshold for frequency is undefined, so it is impossible to know if the Qur'an has reached that level. The second objection points out that similar predictions are frequently made by astrologers and soothsayers, as established by common knowledge and experience, yet these are not considered miraculous by consensus. The third objection states that if only the predictions were miraculous, then the majority of the Qur'an that does not contain predictions would lose its miraculous status, which is untenable.

B) Hearsay and experience

?

?

Third Objection: Most of the Qur'an Lacks Predictions
Third objection: reductio ad absurdum on the scope of i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection: if only prediction-containing verses were miraculous, then most of the Qur'an would lose its attribute of inimitability, which is false.

Core Idea
A miracle can't be limited to a small part of the book.

The third objection is a reductio ad absurdum that targets the logical consequence of limiting the miracle to predictions. If the miraculous nature of the Qur'an depended solely on its predictions of the unseen, then the many verses that do not contain predictions would not be miraculous. This would exclude the majority of the Qur'an from the attribute of inimitability (i'jaz), a conclusion that is clearly false. In this lesson, we examine this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the logical form: if predictions are the miraculous element, then verses without predictions are not miraculous.
The passage ends with 'which is false' indicating why the premise must be rejected.
2
Understand the reductio reasoning
Think about why accepting the premise leads to an unacceptable conclusion. Why is it false that most of the Qur'an is not miraculous?
This relies on the belief that the entire Qur'an is inimitably miraculous.
3
Reflect on the implication
This objection shows that the miraculous nature of the Qur'an must be holistic, not dependent on a single feature like predictions.
Thus the objection strengthens the view that the Qur'an's inimitability is multifaceted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإخبار بالغيب فلوجوه : الأول : أنه جائز كرامة للولي وعلى سبيل الاتفاق أيضا بلا خرق عادة كما في المرة والمرتين (إلا أن يتكرر ) ذلك الإخبار ( إلى أن يصير) خارقاً للعادة فيصير حينئذ ( معجزاً ومراتبه ) أي مراتب التكرر إلى حد الإعجاز (غير مضبوطة) بعدد معين (فكيف يعلم بلوغ القرآن ) في الإخبار بالغيب ( مرتبة الإعجاز . الثاني : أنه يقع ذلك الإخبار مكررا (من المنجمين والكهنة كما دل عليه التسامع والتجربة ) وليس بمعجز اتفاقاً . الثالث : أنه يلزم حينئذ أن لا يكون ما خلا عنه ) أي عن الإخبار بالغيب ( من القرآن معجزاً) فيخرج أكثر القرآن عن صفة الإعجاز وهو باطل
As for predictions of the unseen, there are objections: First: It is permissible as a karamah (miracle) for a saint (wali) and also by coincidence without breaking the norm, as in once or twice (unless that prediction is repeated until it becomes a breach of custom, then it becomes a miracle, and its ranks) i.e., the ranks of repetition to the level of miracle (are not fixed) by a specific number, so how can one know that the Qur'an has reached the level of miraculousness in predicting the unseen? Second: Such predictions occur repeatedly from astrologers and soothsayers, as is known by hearsay and experience, and they are not miraculous by agreement. Third: It would then follow that what is devoid of predictions of the unseen in the Qur'an would not be miraculous, so most of the Qur'an would exit the attribute of inimitability, which is false.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the claim that the Qur'an's predictions of the unseen constitute a miracle. The first objection argues that occasional predictions can be a karamah (divine favor) for a saint or mere coincidence without breaking natural laws; only if predictions become frequent do they break custom and become miracles. However, the threshold for frequency is undefined, so it is impossible to know if the Qur'an has reached that level. The second objection points out that similar predictions are frequently made by astrologers and soothsayers, as established by common knowledge and experience, yet these are not considered miraculous by consensus. The third objection states that if only the predictions were miraculous, then the majority of the Qur'an that does not contain predictions would lose its miraculous status, which is untenable.

Key Terms
إخبار بالغيب
إعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an's miracle is only in its predictions of the unseen.
Correction: The objection shows that if that were true, most of the Qur'an would not be miraculous, which is false; thus the miracle must encompass the entire text.

Checkpoint
What does the third objection claim would happen if predictions were the only source of i'jaz?
A) The entire Qur'an would become more miraculous.
B) Most of the Qur'an would not be considered miraculous.
C) Predictions would become more frequent.
D) Astrologers would stop making predictions.

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that the Qur'an's miracle cannot be limited to predictions; it must be a property of the whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the entire Qur'an as God's inimitable speech, or do you only feel awe for the verses that contain dramatic predictions or miracles? Are you 'downgrading' the rest of the Book?
?
The objector's reasoning shows that a partial miracle is not enough. In your own life, are there areas where you accept only part of God's guidance while ignoring other parts, thinking they are less important?
?
If the Qur'an were only miraculous in its predictions, most of it would lose value. How does this challenge your own reading habits? Are you reading the Qur'an expecting to be amazed by every verse, or do you skip over verses you consider 'ordinary'?
